<p>Hawaii has the highest percentage of Asian Americans and multiracial Americans and the lowest percentage of White Americans of any state. It is the only state where Asian Americans identify as the largest ethnic group. In 2011, 14.5% of births were to white, non-Hispanic parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup> Hawaii's Asian population consists mainly of 198,000 (14.6%) Filipino Americans, 185,000 (13.6%) Japanese Americans, roughly 55,000 (4.0%) Chinese Americans, and 24,000 (1.8%) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_American" title="Korean American">Korean Americans</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> There are over 80,000 Indigenous Hawaiians—5.9% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Census_Bureau_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-US_Census_Bureau-113">[110]</a></sup> Including those with partial ancestry, Samoan Americans constitute 2.8% of Hawaii's population, and Tongan Americans constitute 0.6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_FactFinder_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-American_FactFinder-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over 120,000 (8.8%) of Hispanic and Latino Americans live in Hawaii. Mexican Americans number over 35,000 (2.6%); Puerto Ricans exceed 44,000 (3.2%). Multiracial Americans constitute almost 25% of Hawaii's population, exceeding 320,000 people. Eurasian Americans are a prominent mixed-race group, numbering about 66,000 (4.9%). The Non-Hispanic White population numbers around 310,000—just over 20% of the population. The multi-racial population outnumbers the non-Hispanic white population by about 10,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Census_Bureau_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-US_Census_Bureau-113">[110]</a></sup> In 1970, the Census Bureau reported Hawaii's population was 38.8% white and 57.7% Asian and Pacific Islander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The five largest European ancestries in Hawaii are German (7.4%), Irish (5.2%), English (4.6%), Portuguese (4.3%) and Italian (2.7%). About 82.2% of the state's residents were born in the United States. Roughly 75% of foreign-born residents originate in Asia. Hawaii is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majority-minority_state" title="Majority-minority state">majority-minority state</a>. It was expected to be one of three states that will not have a non-Hispanic white plurality in 2014; the other two are <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="766" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg/220px-Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg/330px-Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg/440px-Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_Japanese_immigrants_to_Hawaii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Japanese immigration to Hawaii was largely fueled by the high demand for plantation labor in Hawaii post-annexation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="629" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg/220px-Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg/330px-Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg/440px-Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portuguese_immigrant_family_in_Hawaii_during_the_19th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Many Portuguese immigrants were <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azorean</a> or <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeiran</a>. They brought with them Roman Catholicism and Portuguese language and cuisine.</div>
</div>

<p>The third group of foreigners to arrive in Hawaii were from China. Chinese workers on Western trading ships settled in Hawaii starting in 1789. In 1820, the first American missionaries arrived to preach Christianity and teach the Hawaiians Western ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charles_Williams_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Williams-118">[115]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hawaii&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, a large proportion of Hawaii's population have Asian ancestry—especially Filipino, Japanese and Chinese. Many are descendants of immigrants brought to work on the sugarcane plantations in the mid-to-late 19th century. The first 153 Japanese immigrants arrived in Hawaii on June 19, 1868. They were not approved by the then-current Japanese government because the contract was between a broker and the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a>—by then replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a>. The first Japanese current-government-approved immigrants arrived on February 9, 1885, after Kalākaua's petition to <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">Emperor Meiji</a> when Kalākaua visited Japan in 1881.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goto_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goto-119">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wao.2BLani_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wao.2BLani-120">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Almost 13,000 Portuguese migrants had arrived by 1899; they also worked on the sugarcane plantations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-121">[118]</a></sup> By 1901, over 5,000 Puerto Ricans were living in Hawaii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L.C3.B3pez_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-L.C3.B3pez-122">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> (<a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">Hawaiian</a> are listed as Hawaii's "official languages" in the state's 1978 constitution. Article XV, Section 4 specifies that "Hawaiian shall be required for public acts and transactions only as provided by law". <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Pidgin" title="Hawaiian Pidgin">Hawai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i Creole English</a>, locally referred to as "Pidgin", is the native language of many native residents and is a second language for many others.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>As of the 2000 Census, 73.4% of Hawaii residents aged five and older exclusively speak English at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mla_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-mla-123">[120]</a></sup> According to the 2008 American Community Survey, 74.6% of Hawaii's residents over the age of five speak only English at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factfinder.census.gov_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-factfinder.census.gov-117">[114]</a></sup> In their homes, 21.0% of state residents speak an additional <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Asia" title="Languages of Asia">Asian language</a>, 2.6% speak Spanish, 1.6% speak other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" title="Indo-European language">Indo-European languages</a> and 0.2% speak another language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factfinder.census.gov_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-factfinder.census.gov-117">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>After English, other languages popularly spoken in the state are <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">Tagalog</a>, Japanese and <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_language" title="Ilocano language">Ilocano</a>. Significant numbers of European immigrants and their descendants also speak their native languages; the most numerous are German, Portuguese, Italian and French.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> 5.4% of residents speak Tagalog—which includes non-native speakers of <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">Filipino language</a>, the national, co-official, Tagalog-based language; 5.0% speak Japanese and 4.0% speak Ilocano; 1.2% speak Chinese, 1.7% speak Hawaiian; 1.7% speak Spanish; 1.6% speak <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>; and 1.0% speak <a href="/wiki/Samoan_language" title="Samoan language">Samoan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mla_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-mla-123">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The keyboard layout used for Hawaiian is <a href="/wiki/QWERTY" title="QWERTY">QWERTY</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hawaiian">Hawaiian</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">Hawaiian language</a></div>
<p>The Hawaiian language has about 2,000 native speakers, about 0.15% of the total population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lyovin_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lyovin-125">[122]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census" title="United States Census">United States Census</a>, there were over 24,000 total speakers of the language in Hawaii in 2006–2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Census_Bureau_Hawaiian_Speaker_estimate_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Census_Bureau_Hawaiian_Speaker_estimate-126">[123]</a></sup> Hawaiian is a Polynesian member of the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian language family</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lyovin_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lyovin-125">[122]</a></sup> It is closely related to other <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Marquesan_language" title="Marquesan language">Marquesan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tahitian_language" title="Tahitian language">Tahitian</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">Māori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_language" title="Rapa Nui language">Rapa Nui</a> (the language of <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a>), and less closely to <a href="/wiki/Samoan_language" title="Samoan language">Samoan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tongan_language" title="Tongan language">Tongan</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>According to Schütz, the Marquesans colonized the archipelago in roughly 300 CE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup> and were later followed by waves of seafarers from the <a href="/wiki/Society_Islands" title="Society Islands">Society Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>These Polynesians remained in the islands; they eventually became the Hawaiian people and <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language#Family_and_origin" title="Hawaiian language">their languages</a> evolved into the Hawaiian language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> Kimura and Wilson say, "[l]inguists agree that Hawaiian is closely related to Eastern Polynesian, with a particularly strong link in the Southern Marquesas, and a secondary link in Tahiti, which may be explained by voyaging between the Hawaiian and Society Islands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup> Before the arrival of Captain James Cook, the Hawaiian language had no written form. That form was developed mainly by American Protestant missionaries between 1820 and 1826. They assigned to the Hawaiian phonemes letters from the Latin alphabet.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Interest in Hawaiian increased significantly in the late 20th century. With the help of the Office of Hawaiian Affairs, specially designated immersion schools in which all subjects would be taught in Hawaiian were established. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_System" title="University of Hawaii System">University of Hawaii</a> developed a Hawaiian language graduate studies program. Municipal codes were altered to favor Hawaiian place and street names for new civic developments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Hawai%27i_Sign_Language" title="Hawai'i Sign Language">Hawai'i Sign Language</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign language</a> for the deaf based on the Hawaiian language, has been in use in the islands since the early 1800s. It is dwindling in numbers due to <a href="/wiki/American_Sign_Language" title="American Sign Language">American Sign Language</a> supplanting HSL through schooling and various other domains.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Hawaiian distinguishes between <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">long and short vowel sounds</a>. In modern practice, vowel length is indicated with a <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language#Orthography_.28writing_system.29" title="Hawaiian language">kahakō</a></i>). Hawaiian-language newspapers (<i>nūpepa</i>) published from 1834 to 1948 and traditional native speakers of Hawaiian generally omit the marks in their own writing. The <span class="unicode">ʻ</span>okina and kahakō are intended to help non-native speakers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Hawaiian language uses the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/%CA%BBOkina" title="ʻOkina"><span class="unicode">ʻ</span>okina</a></i>) as a consonant. It is written as a symbol similar to the apostrophe or left-hanging (opening) single quotation mark.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hawaiian_Pidgin">Hawaiian Pidgin</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Pidgin" title="Hawaiian Pidgin">Hawaiian Pidgin</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hawaii&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(May 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Some residents of Hawaii speak <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Pidgin" title="Hawaiian Pidgin">Hawai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i Creole English</a> (HCE), endonymically called <i>pidgin</i> or <i>pidgin English</i>. The lexicon of HCE derives mainly from English but also uses words that have derived from Hawaiian, Chinese, Japanese, Portuguese, Ilocano and Tagalog. During the 19th century, the increase in immigration—mainly from China, Japan, Portugal—especially from the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a>, and Spain—catalyzed the development of a hybrid variant of English known to its speakers as <i>pidgin</i>. By the early 20th century, pidgin speakers had children who acquired it as their first language. HCE speakers use some Hawaiian words without those words being considered archaic.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Most place names are retained from Hawaiian, as are some names for plants and animals. For example, tuna fish is often called by its Hawaiian name, <i>ahi</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>HCE speakers have modified the meanings of some English words. For example, "aunty" and "uncle" may either refer to any adult who is a friend or be used to show respect to an elder. <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">Syntax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> follow distinctive rules different from those of General American English. For example, instead of "it is hot today, isn't it?", an HCE speaker would say simply "stay hot, eh?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[d]</a></sup> The term <i><a href="/wiki/Da_kine" title="Da kine">da kine</a></i> is used as a <a href="/wiki/Filler_(linguistics)" title="Filler (linguistics)">filler</a>; a substitute for virtually any word or phrase. During the <a href="/wiki/Surfing" title="Surfing">surfing</a> boom in Hawaii, HCE was influenced by surfer slang. Some HCE expressions, such as <i>brah</i> and <i>da kine</i>, have found their ways elsewhere through surfing communities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church,_829_Pensacola_Street,_Honolulu,_Honolulu_County,_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif"><img alt="The façade of a Christian church in downtown Honolulu." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3832" data-file-width="5278" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church%2C_829_Pensacola_Street%2C_Honolulu%2C_Honolulu_County%2C_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church%2C_829_Pensacola_Street%2C_Honolulu%2C_Honolulu_County%2C_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church%2C_829_Pensacola_Street%2C_Honolulu%2C_Honolulu_County%2C_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church%2C_829_Pensacola_Street%2C_Honolulu%2C_Honolulu_County%2C_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church%2C_829_Pensacola_Street%2C_Honolulu%2C_Honolulu_County%2C_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church%2C_829_Pensacola_Street%2C_Honolulu%2C_Honolulu_County%2C_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Perspective_view_of_northwest_elevation_-_Makiki_Christian_Church,_829_Pensacola_Street,_Honolulu,_Honolulu_County,_HI_HABS_HI-533-1.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Makiki Christian Church in Honolulu heavily draws upon <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Christianity is the most widespread religion in Hawaii. It is mainly represented by various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants">Protestants</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholics" title="Catholics">Catholics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mormons" title="Mormons">Mormons</a>. <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> is the second most popular religion, especially among the archipelago's Japanese community. Unaffilliated account for one-quarter of the population.</p>
<p>The largest denominations by number of adherents were the Catholic Church with 249,619 adherents in 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-131">[127]</a></sup> and the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints with 68,128 adherents in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[128]</a></sup> The third-largest religious group includes all non-denominational churches, with 128 congregations and 32,000 members. The third-largest denominational group is the <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Christ" title="United Church of Christ">United Church of Christ</a>, with 115 congregations and 20,000 members. The Southern Baptist Convention has 108 congregations and 18,000 members in Hawaii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to data provided by religious establishments, religion in Hawaii in 2000 was distributed as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[131]</a></sup></p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<ul>
<li>Christianity: 351,000 (29%)</li>
<li>Buddhism: 110,000 (9%)</li>
<li>Judaism: 10,000 (1%)</li>
<li>Other: 100,000 (10%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[α]</a></sup></li>
<li>Unaffiliated: 650,000 (51%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[β]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-greek;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Other" refers to religions other than Christianity, Buddhism, or Judaism; this group includes <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Daoism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_religion" title="Hawaiian religion">Hawaiian religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shintoism" title="Shintoism">Shintoism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, and other religions.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Unaffiliated" refers to people who do not belong to a congregation; this group includes agnostics, <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deist" title="Deist">deists</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">irreligious</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
