<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBT">LGBT</span></h3>
<p>Hawaii has had a long history of <a href="/wiki/Queer" title="Queer">queer</a> identities. <i>Māhū</i> people, who often traversed gender as defined by Western standards, were a respected group of pre-colonization people who were widely known in society as healers. Another Hawaiian word, <i>aikāne</i>, referred to same-sex relationships. According to journals written by Captain Cook's crew, it is widely believed that many <i>ali<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i</i> engaged in <i>aikāne</i> relationships. Hawaiian scholar <a href="/wiki/Lilikal%C4%81_Kame%CA%BBeleihiwa" title="Lilikalā Kameʻeleihiwa">Lilikalā Kame<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>eleihiwa</a> said, "If you didn't sleep with a man, how could you trust him when you went into battle? How would you know if he was going to be the warrior that would protect you at all costs, if he wasn't your lover?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mahu_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-mahu-142">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2012 poll by Gallup found that Hawaii had the largest proportion of lesbian, gay, bisexual and transgender (LGBT) adults in the U.S., at 5.1%, comprising an estimated adult LGBT population of 53,966 individuals. The number of same-sex couple households in 2010 was 3,239; a 35.5% increase of figures from a decade earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup> In 2013, Hawaii became the fifteenth U.S. state to legalize same-sex marriage; a University of Hawaii researcher said the law may boost tourism by $217 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaii_locations_by_per_capita_income" title="Hawaii locations by per capita income">Hawaii locations by per capita income</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu,_Hawaii,_1907_(CHS-418).jpg"><img alt="In a pineapple field, a laborer stands with his hat in hand." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3961" data-file-width="5193" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu%2C_Hawaii%2C_1907_%28CHS-418%29.jpg/220px-Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu%2C_Hawaii%2C_1907_%28CHS-418%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu%2C_Hawaii%2C_1907_%28CHS-418%29.jpg/330px-Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu%2C_Hawaii%2C_1907_%28CHS-418%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu%2C_Hawaii%2C_1907_%28CHS-418%29.jpg/440px-Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu%2C_Hawaii%2C_1907_%28CHS-418%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pineapple_field_near_Honolulu,_Hawaii,_1907_(CHS-418).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Post-annexation, Hawaii's economy and demographic changes were shaped mostly by the agricultural sector's growth.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare,_oil_on_canvas,_c._1935.JPG"><img alt="A painting of two white women surfing, circa 1935." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2872" data-file-width="2176" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_c._1935.JPG/170px-%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_c._1935.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_c._1935.JPG/255px-%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_c._1935.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_c._1935.JPG/340px-%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_c._1935.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%27Two_Surfer_Girls%27_by_William_Fulton_Soare,_oil_on_canvas,_c._1935.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From the end of World War II onwards, depictions and photographs, such as this, of Hawaii as a tropical, leisure paradise encouraged the growth of tourism in Hawaii, which eventually became the largest industry of the islands.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg"><img alt="An American soldier at Schofield Barracks." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1702" data-file-width="2400" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg/220px-US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg/330px-US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg/440px-US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_111218-N-RI884-097_The_U.S._Pacific_Fleet_Marching_Band_participates_in_a_parade_through_downtown_Waikiki_honoring_Japanese-American_vetera.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The U.S. federal government's spending on Hawaii-stationed personnel, installations and materiel, either directly or through military personnel spending, amounts to Hawaii's second largest source of income, after tourism.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The history of Hawaii's economy can be traced through a succession of dominant industries; <a href="/wiki/Sandalwood" title="Sandalwood">sandalwood</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup> sugarcane, <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a>, the military, tourism and education. Since statehood in 1959, tourism has been the largest industry, contributing 24.3% of the gross state product (GSP) in 1997, despite efforts to diversify. The state's gross output for 2003 was <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$47</span><span class="nowrap"> </span>billion; per capita income for Hawaii residents in 2014 was <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$54,516</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup> Hawaiian exports include food and clothing. These industries play a small role in the Hawaiian economy, due to the shipping distance to viable markets, such as the West Coast of the contiguous U.S. The state's food exports include coffee, macadamia nuts, pineapple, livestock, sugarcane and honey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>By weight, honey bees may be the state's most valuable export.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup> According to the Hawaii Agricultural Statistics Service, agricultural sales were <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$370.9</span><span class="nowrap"> </span>million from diversified agriculture, <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$100.6</span><span class="nowrap"> </span>million from pineapple, and <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$64.3</span><span class="nowrap"> </span>million from sugarcane. Hawaii's relatively consistent climate has attracted the seed industry, which is able to test three generations of crops per year on the islands, compared with one or two on the mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup> Seeds yielded <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$264</span> million in 2012, supporting 1,400 workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ny1310_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-ny1310-152">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of December 2015, the state's unemployment rate was 3.2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup> In 2009, the United States military spent <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$12.2</span><span class="nowrap"> </span>billion in Hawaii, accounting for 18% of spending in the state for that year. 75,000 United States Department of Defense personnel live in Hawaii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup> According to a 2013 study by Phoenix Marketing International, Hawaii had the fourth-largest number of millionaires per capita in the United States, with a ratio of 7.2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span></h3>
<p>Hawaii residents pay the most per person in state taxes in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxes_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-taxes-156">[150]</a></sup> Millions of tourists pay <a href="/wiki/Excise" title="Excise">general excise tax</a> and hotel room tax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxes_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-taxes-156">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hawaii Tax Foundation considers the state's tax burden too high, which it says contributes to higher prices and the perception of an unfriendly business climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxes_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-taxes-156">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Senate" title="Hawaii Senate">State Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Sam_Slom" title="Sam Slom">Sam Slom</a> says state taxes are comparatively higher than other states because the state government handles education, health care, and social services that are usually handled at a county or municipal level in most other states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxes_156-3"><a href="#cite_note-taxes-156">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_of_living">Cost of living</span></h3>
<p>The cost of living in Hawaii, specifically Honolulu, is high compared to that of most major U.S. cities. However, the cost of living in Honolulu is 6.7% lower than in New York City and 3.6% lower than in San Francisco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[151]</a></sup> These numbers may not take into account some costs, such as increased travel costs for flights, additional shipping fees, and the loss of promotional participation opportunities for customers outside the contiguous U.S. While some online stores offer free shipping on orders to Hawaii,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[152]</a></sup> many merchants exclude Hawaii, Alaska, Puerto Rico and certain other U.S. territories.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Electric_Industries" title="Hawaiian Electric Industries">Hawaiian Electric Industries</a>, a privately owned company, provides 95% of the state's population with electricity, mostly from fossil-fuel power stations. Average electricity prices in October 2014 (36.41 cents per kilowatt-hour) were nearly three times the national average (12.58 cents per kilowatt-hour) and 80% higher than the second-highest state, Connecticut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>The median home value in Hawaii in the 2000 U.S. Census was <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$272,700</span>, while the national median home value was <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$119,600</span>. Hawaii home values were the highest of all states, including California with a median home value of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$211,500</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[154]</a></sup> Research from the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Realtors" title="National Association of Realtors">National Association of Realtors</a> places the 2010 median sale price of a single family home in Honolulu, Hawaii, at <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$607,600</span> and the U.S. median sales price at <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$173,200</span>. The sale price of single family homes in Hawaii was the highest of any U.S. city in 2010, just above that of the Silicon Valley area of California (<span style="white-space: nowrap">US$602,000</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hawaii's very high <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_living" title="Cost of living">cost of living</a> is the result of several interwoven factors of the global economy in addition to domestic U.S. government trade policy. Like other regions with desirable weather throughout the year, such as areas of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, Hawaii's residents can be considered to be subject to a "<a href="/wiki/Sunshine_tax" title="Sunshine tax">Sunshine tax</a>". This situation is further exacerbated by the natural factors of geography and world distribution that lead to higher prices for goods due to increased shipping costs, a problem which many <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island states and territories</a> suffer from as well. The situation is compounded even further by what could possibly be the single largest contributor to the high costs of living in Hawaii, a U.S. trade law known as the <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Marine_Act_of_1920" title="Merchant Marine Act of 1920">Jones Act, or the Merchant Marine Act of 1920</a>. This trade regulation prohibits any foreign-flagged ships from carrying cargo between two American ports—a practice known as <a href="/wiki/Cabotage" title="Cabotage">cabotage</a>. Most consumer goods in the United States are manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Outsourcing" title="Outsourcing">outsourced</a> labor in East Asia, then transported by <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ships</a> to ports on the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">U.S. mainland</a>, and Hawaii also receives the same goods. Being located in the central Pacific Ocean, right between major Pacific shipping lanes, it would be very economical to unload Hawaiian-bound goods in <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a>, before continuing on to the mainland. However, this would effectively make the second leg of the voyage between Hawaii and the mainland a domestic route between two American ports. Because most large cargo ships operate under <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_convenience" title="Flag of convenience">foreign "flags of convenience"</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a> or <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>, allowing them to avoid the more stringent, and thus more costly, regulations of developed nations' ports, the domestic leg of the voyage would be disallowed by the Jones Act. Instead, those cargo ships must proceed directly to the West Coast, where distributors <a href="/wiki/Break_bulk_cargo" title="Break bulk cargo">break bulk</a> and transport the Hawaiian-bound, Asian-manufactured goods back across the ocean by U.S.-flagged ships and increasing the length of the voyage by more than 50%. This highly inefficient system of shipping Hawaii's consumer cargo comes at a very hefty price for the average Hawaiian citizen, and makes the cost of living in Hawaii much, much higher than it would otherwise be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keeping_up_with_the_Jones_Act_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keeping_up_with_the_Jones_Act-162">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Law_Restricting_Foreign_Ships_Leads_to_Higher_Gas_Prices_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Law_Restricting_Foreign_Ships_Leads_to_Higher_Gas_Prices-163">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hawaiian consumers ultimately bear the expense of transporting goods imposed by the Jones Act. This law makes Hawaii less competitive than West Coast ports as a shopping destination for tourists from countries with much higher taxes like Japan, even though prices for Asian-manufactured goods should be cheaper because Hawaii is much closer than mainland states to Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Star-Bulletin_on_Jones_Act_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Star-Bulletin_on_Jones_Act-164">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slom_SR11_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slom_SR11-165">[159]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Native_Hawaiians" title="Culture of the Native Hawaiians">Culture of the Native Hawaiians</a></div>
<p>The aboriginal culture of Hawaii is Polynesian. Hawaii represents the northernmost extension of the vast <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_Triangle" title="Polynesian Triangle">Polynesian Triangle</a> of the south and central Pacific Ocean. While traditional Hawaiian culture remains as vestiges in modern Hawaiian society, there are re-enactments of the ceremonies and traditions throughout the islands. Some of these cultural influences, including the popularity (in greatly modified form) of <i><a href="/wiki/Luau" title="Luau">lū<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>au</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hula" title="Hula">hula</a></i>, are strong enough to affect the wider United States.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Hawaii" title="Cuisine of Hawaii">Cuisine of Hawaii</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong,_oil_on_canvas_board,_1880,_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art,_accession_12692.1.JPG"><img alt="A painting of a man carrying taro by a yoke." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2457" data-file-width="1846" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong%2C_oil_on_canvas_board%2C_1880%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_accession_12692.1.JPG/170px-Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong%2C_oil_on_canvas_board%2C_1880%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_accession_12692.1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong%2C_oil_on_canvas_board%2C_1880%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_accession_12692.1.JPG/255px-Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong%2C_oil_on_canvas_board%2C_1880%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_accession_12692.1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong%2C_oil_on_canvas_board%2C_1880%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_accession_12692.1.JPG/340px-Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong%2C_oil_on_canvas_board%2C_1880%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_accession_12692.1.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Man_with_a_Yoke_Carrying_Taro_by_Joseph_Strong,_oil_on_canvas_board,_1880,_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art,_accession_12692.1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Taro" title="Taro">Taro</a>, or in Hawaiian <i>kalo</i>, was one of the primary staples in Ancient Hawaii and remains a central ingredient in Hawaiian gastronomy today.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Hawaii" title="Cuisine of Hawaii">cuisine of Hawaii</a> is a fusion of many foods brought by immigrants to the Hawaiian Islands, including the earliest Polynesians and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_Hawaiian_cuisine" title="Native Hawaiian cuisine">Native Hawaiian cuisine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_the_United_States" title="Cuisine of the United States">American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cuisine" title="Chinese cuisine">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippine_cuisine" title="Philippine cuisine">Filipino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_cuisine" title="Korean cuisine">Korean</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_cuisine" title="Polynesian cuisine">Polynesian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_cuisine" title="Portuguese cuisine">Portuguese</a> origins. Plant and animal food sources are imported from around the world for agricultural use in Hawaii. <i><a href="/wiki/Poi_(food)" title="Poi (food)">Poi</a></i>, a starch made by pounding <a href="/wiki/Taro" title="Taro">taro</a>, is one of the traditional foods of the islands. Many local restaurants serve the ubiquitous <a href="/wiki/Plate_lunch" title="Plate lunch">plate lunch</a>, which features two scoops of rice, a simplified version of American <a href="/wiki/Macaroni_salad" title="Macaroni salad">macaroni salad</a> and a variety of toppings including <a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburger</a> patties, a fried egg, and <a href="/wiki/Gravy" title="Gravy">gravy</a> of a <i><a href="/wiki/Loco_moco" title="Loco moco">loco moco</a></i>, Japanese style <i><a href="/wiki/Tonkatsu" title="Tonkatsu">tonkatsu</a></i> or the traditional lū<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>au favorites, including <i><a href="/wiki/Kalua" title="Kalua">kālua</a></i> pork and <i><a href="/wiki/Laulau" title="Laulau">laulau</a></i>. <i><a href="/wiki/Spam_musubi" title="Spam musubi">Spam musubi</a></i> is an example of the fusion of ethnic cuisine that developed on the islands among the mix of immigrant groups and military personnel. In the 1990s, a group of chefs developed <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_regional_cuisine" title="Hawaii regional cuisine">Hawaii regional cuisine</a> as a contemporary fusion cuisine.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Customs_and_etiquette">Customs and etiquette</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Customs_and_etiquette_in_Hawaii" title="Customs and etiquette in Hawaii">Customs and etiquette in Hawaii</a></div>
<p>Some key customs and etiquette in Hawaii are as follows: when visiting a home, it is considered good manners to bring a small gift for one's host (for example, a dessert). Thus, parties are usually in the form of potlucks. Most locals take their shoes off before entering a home. It is customary for Hawaiian families, regardless of ethnicity, to hold a luau to celebrate a child's first birthday. It is also customary at Hawaiian weddings, especially at Filipino weddings, for the bride and groom to do a money dance (also called the <a href="/wiki/Pandanggo" title="Pandanggo">pandanggo</a>). Print media and local residents recommend that one refer to non-Hawaiians as "locals of Hawaii" or "people of Hawaii".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hawaiian_mythology">Hawaiian mythology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg/170px-Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg/255px-Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg/340px-Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethnologisches_Museum_Dahlem_Berlin_Mai_2006_009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A stone carving of a Hawaiian deity, housed at a German museum.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaiian_mythology" title="Hawaiian mythology">Hawaiian mythology</a></div>
<p>Hawaiian mythology comprises the legends, historical tales, and sayings of the ancient Hawaiian people. It is considered a variant of a more general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_mythology" title="Polynesian mythology">Polynesian mythology</a> that developed a unique character for several centuries before <i>circa</i> 1800. It is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_religion" title="Hawaiian religion">Hawaiian religion</a>, which was officially suppressed in the 19th century but was kept alive by some practitioners to the modern day.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Prominent figures and terms include <a href="/wiki/Aumakua" title="Aumakua">Aumakua</a>, the spirit of an ancestor or family god and <a href="/wiki/K%C4%81ne" title="Kāne">Kāne</a>, the highest of the four major Hawaiian deities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polynesian_mythology">Polynesian mythology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_mythology" title="Polynesian mythology">Polynesian mythology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tahiti-Oro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="378" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tahiti-Oro.jpg/170px-Tahiti-Oro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tahiti-Oro.jpg/255px-Tahiti-Oro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tahiti-Oro.jpg/340px-Tahiti-Oro.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tahiti-Oro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sacred god figure wrapping for the war god <a href="/wiki/%27Oro" title="'Oro">'Oro</a>, made of woven dried coconut fibre (<a href="/wiki/Sennit" title="Sennit">sennit</a>), which would have protected a Polynesian god effigy (<i>to'o</i>), made of wood.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Polynesian mythology is the <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral traditions</a> of the people of Polynesia, a grouping of Central and South Pacific Ocean island <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_triangle" title="Polynesian triangle">Polynesian triangle</a> together with the scattered cultures known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_outliers" title="Polynesian outliers">Polynesian outliers</a>. Polynesians speak languages that descend from a language reconstructed as <a href="/wiki/Proto-Polynesian_language" title="Proto-Polynesian language">Proto-Polynesian</a> that was probably spoken in the area around Tonga and Samoa in around 1000 BCE.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Prior to the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_culture" title="Polynesian culture">Polynesian people</a> migrated east to the <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands" title="Cook Islands">Cook Islands</a>, and from there to other island groups such as Tahiti and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquesas" title="Marquesas">Marquesas</a>. Their descendants later discovered the islands Tahiti, Rapa Nui and later the Hawaiian Islands and New Zealand.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The Polynesian languages are part of the Austronesian language family. Many are close enough in terms of vocabulary and grammar to be <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a>. There are also substantial cultural similarities between the various groups, especially in terms of social organization, childrearing, horticulture, building and textile technologies. Their mythologies in particular demonstrate local reworkings of commonly shared tales. The Polynesian cultures each have distinct but related oral traditions; legends or myths are traditionally considered to recount ancient history (the time of "pō") and the adventures of gods ("<a href="/wiki/Atua" title="Atua">atua</a>") and deified ancestors.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_state_parks">List of state parks</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Hawaiian_state_parks" title="List of Hawaiian state parks">List of Hawaiian state parks</a></div>
<p>There are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Hawaiian_state_parks" title="List of Hawaiian state parks">many Hawaiian state parks</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_(island)" title="Hawaii (island)">Island of Hawai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i</a> has state parks, recreation areas, and historical parks.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kauai" title="Kauai">Kaua<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i</a> has the <a href="/wiki/Ahukini_State_Recreation_Pier" title="Ahukini State Recreation Pier">Ahukini State Recreation Pier</a>, six state parks, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Fort_Elizabeth_State_Historical_Park" title="Russian Fort Elizabeth State Historical Park">Russian Fort Elizabeth State Historical Park</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maui" title="Maui">Maui</a> has two state monuments, several state parks, and the <a href="/wiki/Polipoli_Spring_State_Recreation_Area" title="Polipoli Spring State Recreation Area">Polipoli Spring State Recreation Area</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moloka%E2%80%98i" title="Moloka‘i">Moloka‘i</a> has the <a href="/wiki/Pala%27au_State_Park" title="Pala'au State Park">Pala'au State Park</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oahu" title="Oahu">O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu</a> has several state parks, a number of state recreation areas, and a number of monuments, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulu_P%C5%8D_Heiau_State_Monument" title="Ulu Pō Heiau State Monument">Ulu Pō Heiau State Monument</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literature_in_Hawaii" title="Literature in Hawaii">Literature in Hawaii</a></div>
<p>The literature of Hawaii is diverse and includes authors <a href="/wiki/Kiana_Davenport" title="Kiana Davenport">Kiana Davenport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lois-Ann_Yamanaka" title="Lois-Ann Yamanaka">Lois-Ann Yamanaka</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kaui_Hart_Hemmings" title="Kaui Hart Hemmings">Kaui Hart Hemmings</a>. Hawaiian magazines include <i><a href="/wiki/Hana_Hou!" title="Hana Hou!">Hana Hou!</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaii_Business_Magazine" title="Hawaii Business Magazine">Hawaii Business Magazine</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Honolulu_(magazine)" title="Honolulu (magazine)">Honolulu</a></i>, among others.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Hawaii" title="Music of Hawaii">Music of Hawaii</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg/220px-Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg/330px-Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg/440px-Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bonnaroo08_jackjohnson2_lg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jack_Johnson_(musician)" title="Jack Johnson (musician)">Jack Johnson</a>, folk rock musician, was born and raised on <a href="/wiki/North_Shore_(Oahu)" title="North Shore (Oahu)">Oahu's North Shore</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg"><img alt="A young man holds a small four-stringed instrument and strums." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg/170px-Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg/255px-Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg/340px-Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ch%C3%A3_das_Caldeiras-Musicien.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A young <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verdean</a> man plays the Portuguese <i><a href="/wiki/Cavaquinho" title="Cavaquinho">cavaquinho</a></i>, a four-stringed instrument from which the <a href="/wiki/Ukulele" title="Ukulele"><span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ukulele</a> is descended.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The music of Hawaii includes traditional and popular styles, ranging from native Hawaiian folk music to modern <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a>. Hawaii's musical contributions to the <a href="/wiki/Music_of_the_United_States" title="Music of the United States">music of the United States</a> are out of proportion to the state's small size. Styles such as <a href="/wiki/Slack-key_guitar" title="Slack-key guitar">slack-key guitar</a> are well-known worldwide, while Hawaiian-tinged music is a frequent part of <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a> soundtracks. Hawaii also made a major contribution to <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country music</a> with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Steel_guitar" title="Steel guitar">steel guitar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unterberger_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unterberger-166">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional Hawaiian folk music is a major part of the state's musical heritage. The Hawaiian people have inhabited the islands for centuries and have retained much of their traditional musical knowledge. Their music is largely religious in nature, and includes chanting and dance music. Hawaiian music has had an enormous impact on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_music" title="Polynesian music">music of other Polynesian islands</a>; according to Peter Manuel, the influence of Hawaiian music a "unifying factor in the development of modern Pacific musics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manuel_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manuel-167">[161]</a></sup> Native Hawaiian musician and Hawaiian sovereignty activist <a href="/wiki/Israel_Kamakawiwo%CA%BBole" title="Israel Kamakawiwoʻole">Israel Kamakawiwoʻole</a>, famous for his medley of "<a href="/wiki/Somewhere_Over_the_Rainbow/What_a_Wonderful_World" title="Somewhere Over the Rainbow/What a Wonderful World">Somewhere Over the Rainbow/What a Wonderful World</a>", was named "The Voice of Hawaii" by <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> in 2010 in its 50 great voices series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Hawaii" title="Tourism in Hawaii">Tourism in Hawaii</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Punaluu_Beach_Park,_Big_Island,_Hawaii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="380" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Punaluu_Beach_Park%2C_Big_Island%2C_Hawaii.jpg/220px-Punaluu_Beach_Park%2C_Big_Island%2C_Hawaii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Punaluu_Beach_Park%2C_Big_Island%2C_Hawaii.jpg/330px-Punaluu_Beach_Park%2C_Big_Island%2C_Hawaii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/Punaluu_Beach_Park%2C_Big_Island%2C_Hawaii.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Punaluu_Beach_Park,_Big_Island,_Hawaii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Punalu%27u_Beach" title="Punalu'u Beach">Punalu'u Beach</a> on the Big Island. <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Hawaii" title="Tourism in Hawaii">Tourism</a> is Hawaii's leading employer.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tourism is an important part of the Hawaiian economy. In 2003, according to state government data, there were over 6.4 million visitors, with expenditures of over $10 billion, to the Hawaiian Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBEDT_2003_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-DBEDT_2003-169">[163]</a></sup> Due to the mild year-round weather, tourist travel is popular throughout the year. The major holidays are the most popular times for outsiders to visit, especially in the winter months. Substantial numbers of Japanese tourists still visit the islands but have now been surpassed by Chinese and Koreans due to the collapse of the value of the Yen and the weak Japanese economy. The average Japanese stays only 5 days while other Asians spend over 9.5 days and spend 25% more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[164]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Hawaii hosts numerous cultural events. The annual <a href="/wiki/Merrie_Monarch_Festival" title="Merrie Monarch Festival">Merrie Monarch Festival</a> is an international Hula competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[165]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_International_Film_Festival" title="Hawaii International Film Festival">Hawaii International Film Festival</a> is the premier film festival for Pacific rim cinema.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shane_Nelson_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shane_Nelson-172">[166]</a></sup> Honolulu hosts the state's long-running LGBT film festival, the <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_Film_Festival" title="Rainbow Film Festival">Rainbow Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Prepaid_Health_Care_Act" title="Hawaii Prepaid Health Care Act">Hawaii Prepaid Health Care Act</a></div>
<p>As of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hawaii&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Hawaii's health care system insures 92% of residents. Under the state's plan, businesses are required to provide insurance to employees who work more than twenty hours per week. Heavy regulation of insurance companies helps reduce the cost to employers. Due in part to heavy emphasis on preventive care, Hawaiians require hospital treatment less frequently than the rest of the United States, while total health care expenses measured as a percentage of state GDP are substantially lower.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Proponents of <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> elsewhere in the U.S. sometimes use Hawaii as a model for proposed federal and state health care plans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_schools">Public schools</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hawai%27i_Department_of_Education" title="Hawai'i Department of Education">Hawai'i Department of Education</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_elementary_schools_in_Hawaii" title="List of elementary schools in Hawaii">List of elementary schools in Hawaii</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_middle_schools_in_Hawaii" title="List of middle schools in Hawaii">List of middle schools in Hawaii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_high_schools_in_Hawaii" title="List of high schools in Hawaii">List of high schools in Hawaii</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waianae_High_School_(5888481033).jpg"><img alt="Façade of a public high school." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="1600" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Waianae_High_School_%285888481033%29.jpg/220px-Waianae_High_School_%285888481033%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Waianae_High_School_%285888481033%29.jpg/330px-Waianae_High_School_%285888481033%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Waianae_High_School_%285888481033%29.jpg/440px-Waianae_High_School_%285888481033%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waianae_High_School_(5888481033).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Waianae High School, located in <a href="/wiki/Waianae,_Hawaii" title="Waianae, Hawaii">Wai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>anae</a>, houses an educational community media center.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hawaii has the only school system within the U.S. that is unified statewide. Policy decisions are made by the fourteen-member state <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Board_of_Education" title="Hawaii Board of Education">Board of Education</a>, which sets policy and hires the superintendent of schools, who oversees the state <a href="/wiki/Hawai%27i_Department_of_Education" title="Hawai'i Department of Education">Department of Education</a>. The Department of Education is divided into seven districts; four on O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu and one for each of the other three counties. The main rationale for centralization is to combat inequalities between highly populated O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu and the more rural Neighbor Islands, and between lower-income and more affluent areas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Public elementary, middle and high school test scores in Hawaii are below national averages on tests mandated under the <a href="/wiki/No_Child_Left_Behind_Act" title="No Child Left Behind Act">No Child Left Behind Act</a>. The Hawaii Board of Education requires all eligible students to take these tests and report all student test scores. This may have unbalanced the results that reported in August 2005 that of 282 schools across the state, 185 failed to reach federal minimum performance standards in mathematics and reading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACT_(examination)" title="ACT (examination)">ACT college placement tests</a> show that in 2005, seniors scored slightly above the national average (21.9 compared with 20.9),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[170]</a></sup> but in the widely accepted <a href="/wiki/SAT" title="SAT">SAT</a> examinations, Hawaii's college-bound seniors tend to score below the national average in all categories except mathematics.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_schools">Private schools</span></h3>
<p>Hawaii has the highest rates of private school attendance in the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boehonbcn_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-boehonbcn-12">[11]</a></sup> During the 2011–2012 school year, Hawaii public and charter schools had an enrollment of 181,213,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOE_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-177">[171]</a></sup> while private schools had 37,695.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HAIS_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-HAIS-178">[172]</a></sup> Private schools educated over 17% of students in Hawaii that school year, nearly three times the approximate national average of 6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCES_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCES-179">[173]</a></sup> It has four of the largest independent schools; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iolani_School" title="Iolani School"><span class="unicode">ʻ</span>Iolani School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamehameha_Schools" title="Kamehameha Schools">Kamehameha Schools</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mid-Pacific_Institute" title="Mid-Pacific Institute">Mid-Pacific Institute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Punahou_School" title="Punahou School">Punahou School</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Buddhist_Academy" title="Pacific Buddhist Academy">Pacific Buddhist Academy</a>, the second Buddhist high school in the U.S. and first such school in Hawaii, was founded in 2003. The first native controlled public charter school was the <a href="/wiki/Kanu_O_Ka_Aina_New_Century_Charter_School" title="Kanu O Ka Aina New Century Charter School">Kanu O Ka Aina New Century Charter School</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Independent and charter schools can select their students, while the public schools are open to all students in their district. The Kamehameha Schools are the only schools in the U.S. that openly grant admission to students based on ancestry; collectively, they are one of the wealthiest schools in the United States, if not the world, having over eleven billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_dollars" title="US dollars">US dollars</a> in estate assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[174]</a></sup> In 2005, Kamehameha enrolled 5,398 students, 8.4% of the Native Hawaiian children in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleges_and_universities">Colleges and universities</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_Hawaii" title="List of colleges and universities in Hawaii">List of colleges and universities in Hawaii</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1952" data-file-width="3264" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg/220px-University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg/330px-University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg/440px-University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Main Entrance of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo" title="University of Hawaii at Hilo">University of Hawaii at Hilo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Graduates of secondary schools in Hawaii often enter directly into the workforce. Some attend colleges and universities on the mainland or other countries, and the rest attend an institution of higher learning in Hawaii. The largest is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_System" title="University of Hawaii System">University of Hawaii System</a>, which consists of: the research university at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_at_Manoa" title="University of Hawaii at Manoa">Mānoa</a>, two comprehensive campuses at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_at_Hilo" title="University of Hawaii at Hilo">Hilo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii-West_Oahu" title="University of Hawaii-West Oahu">West O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu</a>, and seven community colleges. Private universities include <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young_University%E2%80%93Hawaii" title="Brigham Young University–Hawaii">Brigham Young University–Hawaii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaminade_University_of_Honolulu" title="Chaminade University of Honolulu">Chaminade University of Honolulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Pacific_University" title="Hawaii Pacific University">Hawaii Pacific University</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wayland_Baptist_University" title="Wayland Baptist University">Wayland Baptist University</a>. <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen_Diocesan_Seminary,_Honolulu" title="Saint Stephen Diocesan Seminary, Honolulu">Saint Stephen Diocesan Center</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Seminary" title="Seminary">seminary</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Honolulu" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Honolulu">Roman Catholic Diocese of Honolulu</a>. Kona hosts the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Nations" title="University of the Nations">University of the Nations</a>, which is not an <a href="/wiki/Educational_accreditation" title="Educational accreditation">accredited</a> university.</p>
<h3><span id="Pūnana_Leo"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="P.C5.ABnana_Leo">Pūnana Leo</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/P%C5%ABnana_Leo" title="Pūnana Leo">Pūnana Leo</a></div>
<p>First opened in 1984 illegally in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kekaha" title="Kekaha">Kekaha</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaua%27i" title="Kaua'i">Kaua'i</a>, the Pūnana Leo or "Language Nest" (lit. "Nest of Voices") were the first indigenous language immersion schools in the United States. Modelled after the Māori language <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kohanga_reo" title="Kohanga reo">Kōhanga reo</a> of New Zealand, they provide preschool aged children the opportunity to engage in early education through a Hawaiian language medium, generally taught by elders. Graduates from the Pūnana Leo schools have achieved several measures of academic success in later life. As of 2006, there were a total of eleven Pūnana Leo preschools, with locations on five of the islands.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Department_of_Transportation" title="Hawaii Department of Transportation">Hawaii Department of Transportation</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Hawaii" title="List of airports in Hawaii">List of airports in Hawaii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aviation_in_Hawaii" title="Aviation in Hawaii">Aviation in Hawaii</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1275" data-file-width="1650" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg/220px-HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg/330px-HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg/440px-HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HonoluluAirportWelcomeSign.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The main welcome sign for Honolulu Airport.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Hawaii_state_highways" title="List of Hawaii state highways">system of state highways</a> encircles each main island. Only O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu has federal highways, and is the only area outside the contiguous 48 states to have signed <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate highways</a>. Narrow, winding roads and congestion in populated places can slow traffic. Each major island has a public bus system.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honolulu_International_Airport" title="Honolulu International Airport">Honolulu International Airport</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Air_Transport_Association_airport_code" title="International Air Transport Association airport code">IATA</a>:<span class="nowrap"> </span>HNL), which shares runways with the adjacent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hickam_Field" title="Hickam Field">Hickam Field</a> (IATA:<span class="nowrap"> </span>HIK), is the major commercial aviation hub of Hawaii. The commercial aviation airport offers intercontinental service to North America, Asia, Australia and Oceania. <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Airlines" title="Hawaiian Airlines">Hawaiian Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mokulele_Airlines" title="Mokulele Airlines">Mokulele Airlines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Go!_(airline)" title="Go! (airline)">go!</a> use jets to provide services between the large airports in Honolulu, Līhu<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>e, Kahului, Kona and Hilo. <a href="/wiki/Island_Air_(Hawaii)" title="Island Air (Hawaii)">Island Air</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Wings" title="Pacific Wings">Pacific Wings</a> serve smaller airports. These airlines also provide air freight services between the islands. On May 30, 2017, the airport was officially renamed as the <a href="/wiki/Daniel_K._Inouye_International_Airport" title="Daniel K. Inouye International Airport">Daniel K. Inouye International Airport</a> (HNL), after U.S. Senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Inouye" title="Daniel Inouye">Daniel K. Inouye</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until air passenger services began in the 1920s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawaii_Aviation_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawaii_Aviation-183">[177]</a></sup> private boats were the sole means of traveling between the islands. Seaflite operated hydrofoils between the major islands in the mid-1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Superferry" title="Hawaii Superferry">Hawaii Superferry</a> operated between O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu and Maui between December 2007 and March 2009, with additional routes planned for other islands. Protests and legal problems over environmental impact statements ended the service, though the company operating Superferry has expressed a wish to recommence ferry services in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alakai_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alakai-185">[179]</a></sup> Currently there are passenger ferry services in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maui_County" title="Maui County">Maui County</a> between Moloka<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i and Maui,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molokai_Ferry_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molokai_Ferry-186">[180]</a></sup> and between Lana<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i and Maui,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lana.27i_Ferry_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lana.27i_Ferry-187">[181]</a></sup> though neither of these take vehicles. Currently <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norwegian_Cruise_Lines" title="Norwegian Cruise Lines">Norwegian Cruise Lines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Princess_Cruises" title="Princess Cruises">Princess Cruises</a> provide passenger cruise ship services between the larger islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCL_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCL-188">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Princess_Cruises_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Princess_Cruises-189">[183]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span></h3>
<p>At one time Hawaii had a network of railroads on each of the larger islands that transported farm commodities and passengers. Most were <span class="nowrap">3 ft</span> (<span class="nowrap">914 mm</span>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narrow_gauge" title="Narrow gauge">narrow gauge</a> systems but there were some <span class="nowrap">2 ft 6 in</span> (<span class="nowrap">762 mm</span>) gauge on some of the smaller islands. The standard gauge in the U.S. is <span class="nowrap">4 ft <span class="frac">8<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> in</span> (<span class="nowrap">1,435 mm</span>). By far the largest railroad was the <a href="/wiki/Oahu_Railway_and_Land_Company" title="Oahu Railway and Land Company">Oahu Railway and Land Company</a> (OR&amp;L) that ran lines from Honolulu across the western and northern part of Oahu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-190">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>The OR&amp;L was important for moving troops and goods during World War II. Traffic on this line was busy enough for signals to be used to facilitate movement of trains and to require <a href="/wiki/Wigwag_(railroad)" title="Wigwag (railroad)">wigwag</a> signals at some railroad crossings for the protection of motorists. The main line was officially abandoned in 1947, although part of it was bought by the U.S. Navy and operated until 1970. Thirteen miles (21 km) of track remain; preservationists occasionally run trains over a portion of this line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-190">[184]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honolulu_High-Capacity_Transit_Corridor_Project" title="Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project">Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project</a> aims to add elevated passenger rail on Oahu to relieve highway congestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boehonbcn_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-boehonbcn-12">[11]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_subdivisions_and_local_government">Political subdivisions and local government</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_Hawaii" title="List of counties in Hawaii">List of counties in Hawaii</a></div>
<p>The movement of the Hawaiian royal family from Hawai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i Island to Maui, and subsequently to O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu, explains the modern-day distribution of population centers. <a href="/wiki/Kamehameha_III" title="Kamehameha III">Kamehameha III</a> chose the largest city, Honolulu, as his capital because of its natural harbor—the present-day <a href="/wiki/Honolulu_Harbor" title="Honolulu Harbor">Honolulu Harbor</a>. Now the state capital, Honolulu is located along the southeast coast of O<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ahu. The previous capital was <a href="/wiki/Lahaina,_Hawaii" title="Lahaina, Hawaii">Lahaina</a>, Maui, and before that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kailua-Kona" title="Kailua-Kona">Kailua-Kona</a>, Hawai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i. Some major towns are <a href="/wiki/Hilo,_Hawaii" title="Hilo, Hawaii">Hilo</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%81ne%27ohe,_Hawai%27i" title="Kāne'ohe, Hawai'i">Kāne<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>ohe</a>; <a href="/wiki/Kailua,_Honolulu_County,_Hawaii" title="Kailua, Honolulu County, Hawaii">Kailua</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pearl_City,_Hawaii" title="Pearl City, Hawaii">Pearl City</a>; <a href="/wiki/Waipahu,_Hawaii" title="Waipahu, Hawaii">Waipahu</a>; <a href="/wiki/Kahului,_Hawaii" title="Kahului, Hawaii">Kahului</a>; <a href="/wiki/Kailua,_Hawaii_County,_Hawaii" title="Kailua, Hawaii County, Hawaii">Kailua-Kona</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kihei,_Hawaii" title="Kihei, Hawaii">Kīhei</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Lihue,_Hawaii" title="Lihue, Hawaii">Līhu<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>e</a>.</p>
<p>Hawaii comprises five counties: the <a href="/wiki/Honolulu_County,_Hawaii" title="Honolulu County, Hawaii">City and County of Honolulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_County,_Hawaii" title="Hawaii County, Hawaii">Hawaii County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maui_County,_Hawaii" title="Maui County, Hawaii">Maui County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kauai_County,_Hawaii" title="Kauai County, Hawaii">Kauai County</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kalawao_County,_Hawaii" title="Kalawao County, Hawaii">Kalawao County</a>.</p>
<p>Hawaii has the fewest local governments among U.S. states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCensus2010_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-USCensus2010-191">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCensus2007_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-USCensus2007-192">[186]</a></sup> Unique to this state is the lack of <a href="/wiki/Municipal_corporation" title="Municipal corporation">municipal governments</a>. All local governments are generally administered at the <a href="/wiki/County_(United_States)" title="County (United States)">county</a> level. The only incorporated area in the state is <a href="/wiki/Honolulu_County,_Hawaii" title="Honolulu County, Hawaii">Honolulu County</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consolidated_city%E2%80%93county" title="Consolidated city–county">consolidated city–county</a> that governs the entire island of Oahu. County executives are referred to as mayors; these are the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Hawaii_County" title="Mayor of Hawaii County">Mayor of Hawaii County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Honolulu" title="Mayor of Honolulu">Mayor of Honolulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Kauai" title="Mayor of Kauai">Mayor of Kaua<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Maui" title="Mayor of Maui">Mayor of Maui</a>. The mayors are all elected in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonpartisan" title="Nonpartisan">nonpartisan</a> elections. Kalawao County has no elected government,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[187]</a></sup> and as <a href="#Education">mentioned above</a> there are no local <a href="/wiki/School_district" title="School district">school districts</a> and instead all local public education is administered at the state level by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaii_Department_of_Education" title="Hawaii Department of Education">Hawaii Department of Education</a>. The remaining local governments are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special-purpose_district" title="Special-purpose district">special districts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCensus2010_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-USCensus2010-191">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCensus2007_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-USCensus2007-192">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_government">State government</span></h3>
<p>The state government of Hawaii is modeled after the federal government with adaptations originating from the kingdom era of Hawaiian history. As codified in the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Hawaii" title="Constitution of Hawaii">Constitution of Hawaii</a>, there are three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Branches_of_government" title="Branches of government">branches of government</a>: executive, legislative and judicial. The executive branch is led by the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Hawaii" title="Governor of Hawaii">Governor of Hawaii</a>, who is assisted by the <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_Hawaii" title="Lieutenant Governor of Hawaii">Lieutenant Governor of Hawaii</a>, both of whom are elected on the same ticket. The governor is the only state public official elected statewide; all others are appointed by the governor. The lieutenant governor acts as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_of_Hawaii" title="Secretary of State of Hawaii">Secretary of State</a>. The governor and lieutenant governor oversee twenty agencies and departments from offices in the <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_State_Capitol" title="Hawaii State Capitol">State Capitol</a>. The official residence of the governor is <a href="/wiki/Washington_Place" title="Washington Place">Washington Place</a>.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Category:State_agencies_of_Hawaii" title="Category:State agencies of Hawaii">Category:State agencies of Hawaii</a></div>
<p>The legislative branch consists of the <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_State_Legislature" title="Hawaii State Legislature">Hawaii State Legislature</a>, which is composed of the 51-member <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_House_of_Representatives" title="Hawaii House of Representatives">Hawaii House of Representatives</a> led by the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_(politics)" title="Speaker (politics)">Speaker of the House</a>, and the 25-member <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Senate" title="Hawaii Senate">Hawaii Senate</a> led by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Senate" title="President of the Senate">President of the Senate</a>. The Legislature meets at the State Capitol. The unified judicial branch of Hawaii is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaii_State_Judiciary" title="Hawaii State Judiciary">Hawaii State Judiciary</a>. The <a href="/wiki/State_supreme_court" title="State supreme court">state's highest court</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Hawaii" title="Supreme Court of Hawaii">Supreme Court of Hawaii</a>, which uses <a href="/wiki/Aliiolani_Hale" title="Aliiolani Hale">Ali<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>iōlani Hale</a> as its chambers.</p>

<p>Hawaii is represented in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> by two senators and two <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">representatives</a>. As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hawaii&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, all four seats are held by Democrats. <a href="/wiki/Colleen_Hanabusa" title="Colleen Hanabusa">Colleen Hanabusa</a> won a special election for the <a href="/wiki/Hawaii%27s_1st_congressional_district" title="Hawaii's 1st congressional district">1st congressional district</a> representing southeastern Oahu, including central Honolulu, on November 8, 2016 to finish the term of Rep. <a href="/wiki/Mark_Takai" title="Mark Takai">Mark Takai</a> who died July 20, 2016. <a href="/wiki/Tulsi_Gabbard" title="Tulsi Gabbard">Tulsi Gabbard</a> represents the <a href="/wiki/Hawaii%27s_2nd_congressional_district" title="Hawaii's 2nd congressional district">2nd congressional district</a>, representing the rest of the state, which is largely rural and semi-rural.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Brian_Schatz" title="Brian Schatz">Brian Schatz</a> is the senior United States Senator from Hawaii. He was appointed to the office on December 26, 2012, by Governor <a href="/wiki/Neil_Abercrombie" title="Neil Abercrombie">Neil Abercrombie</a>, following the death of former senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Inouye" title="Daniel Inouye">Daniel Inouye</a>. The state's junior senator is <a href="/wiki/Mazie_Hirono" title="Mazie Hirono">Mazie Hirono</a>, the former representative from the second congressional district. Hirono is the first female Asian American senator and the first Buddhist senator. Hawaii incurred the biggest <a href="/wiki/Seniority_in_the_United_States_Senate" title="Seniority in the United States Senate">seniority</a> shift between the <a href="/wiki/112th_United_States_Congress" title="112th United States Congress">112th</a> and <a href="/wiki/113th_United_States_Congress" title="113th United States Congress">113th</a> Congresses. The state went from a delegation consisting of senators who were first and twenty-first in seniority<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[e]</a></sup> to their respective replacements, relative newcomers Schatz and Hirono.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>Federal officials in Hawaii are based at the <a href="/wiki/Prince_Kuhio_Federal_Building" title="Prince Kuhio Federal Building">Prince Kūhiō Federal Building</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Aloha_Tower" title="Aloha Tower">Aloha Tower</a> and Honolulu Harbor. The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> maintain their offices there; the building is also the site of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_federal_courts" title="United States federal courts">federal</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_Hawaii" title="United States District Court for the District of Hawaii">District Court for the District of Hawaii</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_for_the_District_of_Hawaii" title="United States Attorney for the District of Hawaii">United States Attorney for the District of Hawaii</a>.</p>

<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treemap" title="Treemap">Treemap</a> of the popular vote by county, 2016 presidential election.</div>
