</div>
<p>Since gaining statehood and participating in its first election in<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1960" title="United States presidential election, 1960">1960</a>, Hawaii has supported Democrats in all but two presidential elections; <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1972" title="United States presidential election, 1972">1972</a> and<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1984" title="United States presidential election, 1984">1984</a>, both of which were landslide victories for Republicans <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> respectively. In Hawaii's statehood tenure, only Minnesota has supported Republican candidates fewer times in presidential elections.</p>
<p>Hawaii hasn't elected a Republican to represent the state in the U.S. Senate since <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Fong" title="Hiram Fong">Hiram Fong</a> in 1970; since 1977, both of the state's U.S. Senators have been Democrats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2004" title="United States presidential election, 2004">2004</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> won the state's four electoral votes by a margin of nine percentage points with 54% of the vote. Every county supported the Democratic candidate. In 1964, <a href="/wiki/Favorite_son" title="Favorite son">favorite son</a> candidate senator <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Fong" title="Hiram Fong">Hiram Fong</a> of Hawaii sought the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> presidential nomination, while <a href="/wiki/Patsy_Mink" title="Patsy Mink">Patsy Mink</a> ran in the Oregon primary in 1972.</p>
<p>Honolulu-born <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, then serving as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" title="United States Senator">United States Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, was elected the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Presidents" title="List of United States Presidents">44th</a> President of the United States on <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">November 4, 2008</a> and was re-elected for a second term on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Presidential_Election,_2012" title="United States Presidential Election, 2012">November 6, 2012</a>. Obama had won the Hawaii Democratic caucus on February 19, 2008, with 76% of the vote. He was the third Hawaii-born candidate to seek the nomination of a major party and the first presidential nominee from Hawaii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[194]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_status_of_Hawaii">Legal status of Hawaii</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Legal_status_of_Hawaii" title="Legal status of Hawaii">Legal status of Hawaii</a></div>
<p>While Hawaii is internationally recognized as a state of the United States while also being broadly accepted as such in mainstream understanding, the legality of this status has been raised in U.S. District Court,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-district_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-district-202">[195]</a></sup> the U.N., and other international forums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN-forum_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-forum-203">[196]</a></sup> Domestically, the debate is a topic covered in the <a href="/wiki/Kamehameha_Schools" title="Kamehameha Schools">Kamehameha Schools</a> curriculum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curriculum_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-curriculum-204">[197]</a></sup> On September 29, 2015 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_the_Interior" title="Department of the Interior">Department of the Interior</a> announced a procedure to recognize a <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_recognition_of_Native_Hawaiians" title="United States federal recognition of Native Hawaiians">Native Hawaiian government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lauer_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lauer-205">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hawaiian_sovereignty_movement">Hawaiian sovereignty movement</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Hawaiian_sovereignty_movement_groups" title="List of Hawaiian sovereignty movement groups">Hawaiian sovereignty movement</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political" title="Political">Political</a> organizations seeking some form of sovereignty for Hawaii have been active since the 1880s. Generally, their focus is on <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-governance" title="Self-governance">self-governance</a>, either for Hawaii as an independent nation (in many proposals, for "Hawaiian nationals" descended from subjects of the Hawaiian Kingdom or declaring themselves as such by choice), or for people of whole or part <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_Hawaiian" title="Native Hawaiian">native Hawaiian</a> ancestry in an indigenous "<i>nation to nation</i>" relationship akin to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribal_sovereignty" title="Tribal sovereignty">tribal sovereignty</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_federal_recognition_of_Native_Hawaiians" title="US federal recognition of Native Hawaiians">US federal recognition of Native Hawaiians</a>. The movement generally views both the overthrow and annexation as illegal, with the <a href="/wiki/Apology_Resolution" title="Apology Resolution">Apology Resolution</a> passed by US Congress in 1993 cited as a major impetus by the movement for Hawaiian sovereignty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawaii1893_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawaii1893-207">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawaiisov_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawaiisov-208">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[203]</a></sup> A 2005 Grassroot Institute poll found the majority of Hawaiian residents opposed the <a href="/wiki/Akaka_Bill" title="Akaka Bill">Akaka Bill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[204]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Local usage generally reserves <i>Hawaiian</i> as an <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">ethnonym</a> referring to <a href="/wiki/Native_Hawaiians" title="Native Hawaiians">Native Hawaiians</a>. <i>Hawaii resident</i> is the preferred local form to refer to state residents in general regardless of ethnicity. <i>Hawaii</i> may also be used adjectivally. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associated_Press_Stylebook" title="Associated Press Stylebook">Associated Press Stylebook</a></i>, 42nd ed. (2007), also prescribes this usage (p. 112).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BBokina" title="ʻokina">ʻokina</a>, which resembles an apostrophe and precedes the final <i>i</i> in <i>Hawai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i</i>, is a consonant in Hawaiian and phonetically represents the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ʔ/: the catch in 'uh-oh'">ʔ</span></span>/</a></span></span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For comparison, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>—which has 8,717,925 people in 7,417 square miles (19,210 km<sup>2</sup>)—is the most-densely populated state in <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">the Union</a> with 1,134 people per square mile.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">English "to be" is often omitted in Pidgin. In contexts where "to be" is used in <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a>, "to stay" is preferred. "To stay" may have arisen due to an English calque of the Portuguese <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ser#Portuguese" title="wikt:ser">ser</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/estar#Portuguese" title="wikt:estar">estar</a></i>, or <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ficar#Portuguese" title="wikt:ficar">ficar</a></i>. <i>Eh?</i> (<small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[æ̃ː˧˦]</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Tag_question" title="Tag question">tag question</a> which may have roots in Japanese, which utilizes ね (ne?) to emphasize a point that may be agreed upon by all parties, or may come from Portuguese <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/n%C3%A9#Portuguese" title="wikt:né">né?</a> (shortened from "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/n%C3%A3o_%C3%A9#Portuguese" title="wikt:não é">não é?</a>"), cf. French <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/n%27est-ce_pas#French" title="wikt:n'est-ce pas">n'est-ce pas ?</a>. <i>Eh?</i> may also have come from English <i>yeah</i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-195"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-195">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Senator Inouye, who ranked first in seniority, died in December 2012. Senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Akaka" title="Daniel Akaka">Daniel Akaka</a>, who ranked 21st of the Senate's one hundred members, retired in January 2013 after serving twenty-three years in the Senate.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>
